        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Graduate Program</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-13" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h2>The Doctoral Program</h2>
<p>For admission to the doctoral program, students usually will present evidence of sound training in literature written in three languages, one of which may be English. They will be expected to develop a strong enough competence in one of these literatures to be qualified to teach in a national literature department since comparatists are often hired in such departments or have joint appointments.</p>
<p>The major literature is studied in a cross-cultural context linking it with the other two diachronically or synchronically. Students may pursue literary currents or follow the evolution of ideas or themes across linguistic boundaries, or may study features of genre, style, convention, etc. The program may also emphasize theory including poetics, stylistics, semiology, feminist, socio-cultural, post-structuralist, and post-colonial approaches.</p>
<h2>Application Procedure</h2>
<p>The&nbsp;<a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/gradschool/application-information" target="_blank">Graduate School Admission Office</a>&nbsp;employs an&nbsp;<a href="https://www.applyweb.com/browng/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">online application</a>&nbsp;by CollegeNET, the application hosting service affiliated with the school. The deadline for submitting applications for admission with financial aid is December 15. Inquiries may also be addressed to the Director of Graduate Study, Department of Comparative Literature, Box E, Brown University, Providence, Rhode Island 02912 or by&nbsp;<a href="mailto:comp_lit@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">emailing our Department</a>.</p>
<h3>Languages</h3>
<p>The completion of the program requires development of language skills sufficient for advanced work in three literatures. The languages selected are chosen with a view to their appropriateness to the student's areas of special interest. The Department's major competence is in literary expression in western languages. However, our growing programs in classical and modern Chinese, Japanese, and Arabic provide opportunity for students trained in these languages. In addition, the study of both ancient and modern Hebrew is available through Judaic Studies. In at least one of the foreign languages presented, near native proficiency in speaking and writing in the case of a modern language will be expected. See the Language Competence section of the Graduate Procedures for specific requirements.</p>
<h3>Course Work</h3>
<p>Students entering with the B.A. will normally take 15 advanced literature courses and graduate seminars, spread over three years in the proportions respectively of 8, 6, 1, two or three of which may be individual work supervised by a staff member. In special circumstances students may obtain up to a year of course credit for graduate work done at other institutions. Students who enter already having completed some advanced work may be asked to proceed more quickly. Individual programs are worked out in consultation with the Director of Graduate Study to include:</p>
<ul>
<li>a substantial core of graduate seminars whose primary department listing is in Comparative Literature, at least one per semester during the first two years of study</li>
<li>a substantial core of courses primarily in one national literature along with significant related work in at least two others. Courses taken in the second and third literatures must include a minimum of two regularly scheduled graduate seminars (or 100 level where appropriate with approval of the Director of Graduate Study)</li>
<li>a spread of courses comprising work in all three major genres (poetry, drama, narrative) and covering a significant range of distinct cultural epochs (medieval, romantic, modern, and so forth)</li>
<li>some work in the area of literary theory, literary criticism, or literary translation.</li>
<li>if pertinent, courses relating literature to other fields of inquiry or expression; for example, linguistics, philosophy, psychology, history, music, or the visual arts</li>
</ul>
<h3>Comparative Project</h3>
<p>While the historical "coverage" by itself is not the aim of comparative literature, the Department does require a major literature examination just before the fifth semester which has coverage as a partial goal, in order to demonstrate the student's professional capacities in her/his national literature. It also requires a written comparative project, to be submitted in writing and presented orally during the sixth semester. This project allows the student to treat work from more than one literary tradition, and may become part of the dissertation. A topic will be chosen in consultation with a faculty comittee and worked out with the Director of Graduate Study. It is expected that the comparative project will be completed and approved, and the student advanced to doctoral candidacy, by the end of the third year. See Graduate Program Procedures.</p>
<h3>The Dissertation</h3>
<p>By the end of the third year of study the student is expected to select the area of focus for the dissertation. The student will ordinarily work under the close supervision of a member of the Comparative Literature faculty; the thesis must also be approved by two other readers, one of whom may be from outside the Department.</p>
<h3>Teaching Positions</h3>
<p>Training and experience in teaching are major features of our doctoral program in Comparative Literature. The Department makes every attempt to provide its graduate students with teaching experience at Brown in undergraduate courses suited to the graduate student's interest. Teaching assistants work under the direct supervision of members of the professorial staff. At least two years of work as a teaching assistant are required for the Ph.D. The Department will keep students informed of positions as they become available in pertinent departments and at other institutions, and will assist them in presenting their candidacy for such positions.</p>
<p>Advanced graduate students who have made substantial progress on their dissertations, who can document their success in the classroom, and who have the support of a faculty member willing to serve as a classroom mentor are invited to submit a proposal to teach an undergraduate course (below 1000-level) in the department. The proposal consists of a 200-word course description, a syllabus, a C.V. that includes a listing of teaching experience, and notes of support from the dissertation advisor and faculty mentor. It is due to the Director of Graduate Studies no later than October 31 of the academic year before the class is taught. The proposals will be evaluated by the department’s Graduate Committee on their merits; no more than two will be approved in any year; final approval will depend on the curricular needs of the department as determined by the Chair.</p>
<h3>The M.A. Degree</h3>
<p>The Department does not admit terminal M.A. candidates, nor does it require a Master's degree as a prerequisite for the doctorate. Students who are not recommended to proceed in the doctoral program at the end of the first year may take an M.A. by completing one year of full-time course work in residence (normally four courses each semester), satisfying two of the three language requirements for the Ph.D., and completing a Master's thesis consisting of an essay of 50-60 pages on a comparative subject. Alternatively, a student will receive the M.A. upon completion of the comparative project and advancement to doctoral candidacy.</p>
<h2>Financing and Support</h2>
<p>Students are supported by a fellowship in their first year.&nbsp; Teaching responsibilities, and support in the form of a teaching assistantship, begin in the second year and continue through the fourth.&nbsp; We offer a dissertation fellowship in the fifth year and a number of avenues to sixth-year funding, including interdisciplinary opportunities at Brown’s Centers and Institutes and stipend support from the department and the Graduate School.&nbsp; In special cases, the Graduate School may cover tuition and health costs, but not stipend support, beyond the sixth year.&nbsp; Please refer to the&nbsp;<a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/gradschool/financing-support">Graduate School</a>&nbsp;web site for further information about financing graduate study at Brown.</p>
<p><span style="color:#2f2a20">Related Departments and Programs</span></p>
<p>The language and literature offerings at Brown include the following: Classics (including Greek, Latin and Sanskrit), English, French Studies, German Studies, Hebrew, Hispanic Studies, Italian Studies, Portuguese and Brazilian, Arabic, Chinese, Japanese, Linguistics, Slavic Languages.</p>

        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Honors Thesis</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-193" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h3><strong>Honors in Comparative Literature</strong></h3>
<p>Students may earn honors in the concentration by successfully presenting a thesis, for the preparation of which they will normally enroll in COLT 1990 in either or both semesters of their senior year.&nbsp; These courses may not be used to satisfy the standard requirements for a concentration. Honors candidates must also normally earn more A's than B's in courses taken as part of the Comparative Literature concentration (or receive the equivalent faculty evaluation on a Course Performance Report for courses taken S/NC).</p>
<h3><strong>The Honors Thesis</strong></h3>
<p>Students should generally begin thinking about an Honors Thesis in their third year of course work. Students who plan to go abroad in the junior year are advised to give some thought to these matters before leaving; they may also want to write to certain professors from abroad, indicating their potential thesis interests. Optimally, it may be possible to devise a summer reading list for thesis research, so that the project already has some momentum when senior year starts.</p>
<p>The Department expects students to have two readers for the honors thesis, and one of them should normally have some connection with the Comparative Literature department. In addition to discussing possible topics and directions for the thesis, students will want to discuss with the faculty person(s) procedural and technical matters as well. In particular, it is necessary to agree on deadlines for completing readings and submitting drafts, on the amount of consultation that is expected, etc. It is important that all parties have a clear sense of these matters.</p>
<p>&nbsp;Students planning to do honors theses in the department must submit the titles and abstracts of their projects as well as the names and signatures of their directors and second readers by October 15. (<a href="http://brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Revisedhonorsthesisform-2011.doc">Thesis Proposal form</a>)</p>
<p>Most students require two semesters to plan and complete an Honors Thesis. A substantial portion of writing on the thesis must be completed and submitted to the advisor(s) during the Fall semester. The second semester is devoted to completing the writing, and a full draft of the entire thesis must be submitted by March 15.</p>
<p>&nbsp;The final complete version of the thesis must be submitted by April 15. Students should submit one copy to each reader and one electronic <strong>and</strong> one hard copy of the thesis to the Department. Honors in Comparative Literature is granted upon positive recommendation by the thesis advisors.</p>
<p>&nbsp;The honors thesis is usually between 50 and 100 pages long. It is anticipated that a significant amount of research will go into this project. Students are expected normally to choose a topic that involves work in each of the foreign literatures they have presented for their degree; such foreign materials are to be dealt with (i.e., read and cited) in the original language, even though the thesis itself is generally written in English. It is also anticipated that the project itself is to be of a comparative nature. In some cases, it may be possible for a student to devise a thesis project that meshes the thesis investigation with creative writing.</p>
<hr />
<h3><br />Important Dates</h3>
<p><strong>October 15</strong>: Students planning to do honors theses in the department must submit the titles and abstracts of their projects as well as the names and signatures of their directors and second readers.<br /><strong>March 15</strong>: full draft of the entire thesis must be submitted.<br /><strong>April 15</strong>: The final complete version of the thesis must be submitted.</p>
<hr />
<h3><br />Useful Writing and Editing Tools for Honors Thesis Writers</h3>
<ul>
<li><em>How to Write a BA Thesis: A Practical Guide from Your First Ideas to Your Finished Paper</em> by Charles Lipson
<ul>
<li><a href="http://site.ebrary.com.revproxy.brown.edu/lib/brown/Doc?id=10216904" target="_blank">Full text via Brown Library</a></li>
<li><a href="http://www.charleslipson.com/How-to-write-a-thesis.htm" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Online summary</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<ul>
</ul>
<ul>
<li>&nbsp;<a href="http://library.osu.edu/how-to-find/research-strategies/how-do-i-cite-references/mla-citation-guide" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">MLA Citation Guide</a></li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><em><a href="http://www.crockford.com/wrrrld/style.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">The Elements of Style</a></em> by William Strunk, Jr.</li>
</ul>
<ul>
<li><a href="http://english.ttu.edu/kairos/1.2/inbox/mla_archive.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow"><em>Beyond the MLA Handbook: Documenting Electronic Sources on the Internet</em></a> by Andrew Harnack and Gene Kleppinger</li>
</ul>

  <div class="content">
        <h2>Comparative Literature Courses</h2>
<p><strong><span class="small">Course options for </span><span class="small">fulfilling </span><span class="small" style="text-decoration:underline"><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Fall%202017%20REQS_OM_9.15.2017_0.pdf" target="_blank">concentration requirements 2017-2018.</a></span><br /></strong><br /><strong>2017-2018<br /></strong><strong><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/2017%20FALL%20CourseOfferings_9.15.2017.pdf">Fall 2017</a></strong></p>
<p><strong><strong style="color:#000000">2016-2017<br /></strong></strong><strong><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Update%2C%20Reqs.%20Fall%202016%20.pdf" target="_blank">Fall 2016</a></strong><strong>&nbsp;&nbsp;</strong><strong>&nbsp;<br /></strong><strong><strong><a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Reqs.%20Spring%202017_0.pdf" target="_blank">Spring 2017</a></strong></strong></p>
<p align="left"><strong>2015-2016<br /></strong><a href="http://brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/course%20options%20for%20fulfilling%20concentration%20requirements%20Fall%202015.pdf"><strong>Fall 2015</strong></a><strong>&nbsp;</strong><strong>&nbsp;<br /></strong><a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Reqs%20Spring%202016%20%20%282%29.pdf"><strong>Spring 2016</strong></a><strong>&nbsp;</strong></p>
<p align="left"><strong>2014-2014<br /></strong><a href="http://brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Spring%202015%20REQS.pdf"><strong>Spring 2015</strong></a><strong>&nbsp;&nbsp;</strong></p>
  </div>

                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Books by Professors of Comparative Literature</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-298" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <p class="large"><strong>Selections from the Faculty Bookshelf</strong></p>
<p class="large"><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:137px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/images/evdokimova.preview.jpeg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-preview " width="129" height="195" /></span></p>
<p class="p1 small">Svetlana Evdokimova and Vladimir Golstein, eds.&nbsp;<a href="http://www.academicstudiespress.com/browse-catalog/dostoevsky-beyond-dostoevsky" target="_blank" rel="nofollow"><span class="s1">Dostoevsky Beyond Dostoevsky: Science, Religion, Philosophy</span></a>. Brighton, Massachusetts: Academic Studies Press, 2016</p>
<p class="large small roman-lower"><span class="small">.</span></p>
<p class="large">&nbsp;</p>
<p class="large">&nbsp;</p>
<p class="large"><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:135px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/images/9780143107484.preview.jpeg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-preview " width="127" height="195" /></span><span class="small">Shihab al-Din al-Nuwayri, Elias Muhanna trans.,&nbsp;</span><em class="small"><a href="http://www.penguinrandomhouse.com/books/316708/the-ultimate-ambition-in-the-arts-of-erudition-by-shihab-al-din-al-nuwayri-edited-translated-and-with-an-introduction-and-notes-by-elias-muhanna/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">The Ultimate Ambition in the Arts of Erudition</a>.&nbsp;</em><span class="small">New York: Penguin Classics, 2016.&nbsp;</span></p>
<p class="large">&nbsp;</p>
<p class="large">&nbsp;</p>
<p class="large">&nbsp;</p>
<p class="large"><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:134px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/images/image_mini.preview.jpeg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-preview " width="126" height="195" /></span></p>
<p class="large"><span class="small">David Wills,&nbsp;</span><em class="small"><a href="https://www.upress.umn.edu/book-division/books/inanimation" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Inanimation: Theories of Inorganic Life</a>.</em><span class="small">&nbsp;<span>Minneapolis</span>: University of Minnesota Press, 2016.</span><br /><br /></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left"><img class="image image-preview" src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/images/imgres-6.preview.jpg" width="129" height="195" /></span>Marcie Frank, Jonathan Goldberg and Karen Newman eds.,&nbsp;<a href="http://fordhampress.com/index.php/subjects/renaissance-studies/this-distracted-gobe-cloth.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">This Distracted Globe: Worldmaking in Early Modern Literature</a>. New York: Fordham University Press, 2016.</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><br /><br /></p>
<p><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:135px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/images/9783825375652.preview.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-preview " width="127" height="195" /></span></p>
<p><span>Joseph Pucci and Scott McGill, eds.,&nbsp;&nbsp;<a href="https://www.winter-verlag.de/en/detail/978-3-8253-7565-2/McGill_Pucci_Eds_Classics_Renewed_PDF/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">Classics Renewed: Reception and Innovation in the Latin Poetry of Late Antiquity.</a> &nbsp;Heidelberg: Universitätsverlag Winter, 2016.&nbsp;<br /></span></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><br /><br /></p>
<p><span><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:139px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/images/muhanna-digitalhumanities.preview.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-preview " width="131" height="195" /></span>Elias Muhanna ed.,</span><a href="http://www.degruyter.com/view/product/432146?rskey=7Gi04h&amp;result=1" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">The Digital Humanities and Islamic &amp; Middle East Studies</a>.&nbsp;<span>Berlin: De Gruyter, 2016.&nbsp;</span></p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p>&nbsp;</p>
<p><span class="thumbnail-wrapper inline inline-left" style="width:138px;"><img src="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/images/neryda.preview.jpg" alt="" title=""  class="image image-preview " width="130" height="195" /></span><span>Pablo Neruda,&nbsp;</span><span><a href="https://www.coppercanyonpress.org/pages/browse/book.asp?bg=%7B74635BB6-1B61-45B9-A003-A6D8767D6EC1%7D" target="_blank" rel="nofollow"><span>Then Come Back: The Lost Neruda</span></a></span><em><span>.</span></em><span>&nbsp;Translated by Forrest Gander.&nbsp;</span><span>Port Townsend, Washington&nbsp;</span><span>: Copper Canyon Press, 2016.</span></p>
<div></div>
<div></div>
<div></div>
<div></div>
<div></div>
<div></div>

<p>&nbsp;</p>

                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Graduate Program</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-13" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h2>The Doctoral Program</h2>
<p>For admission to the doctoral program, students usually will present evidence of sound training in literature written in three languages, one of which may be English. They will be expected to develop a strong enough competence in one of these literatures to be qualified to teach in a national literature department since comparatists are often hired in such departments or have joint appointments.</p>
<p>The major literature is studied in a cross-cultural context linking it with the other two diachronically or synchronically. Students may pursue literary currents or follow the evolution of ideas or themes across linguistic boundaries, or may study features of genre, style, convention, etc. The program may also emphasize theory including poetics, stylistics, semiology, feminist, socio-cultural, post-structuralist, and post-colonial approaches.</p>
<h2>Application Procedure</h2>
<p>The&nbsp;<a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/gradschool/application-information" target="_blank">Graduate School Admission Office</a>&nbsp;employs an&nbsp;<a href="https://www.applyweb.com/browng/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow">online application</a>&nbsp;by CollegeNET, the application hosting service affiliated with the school. The deadline for submitting applications for admission with financial aid is December 15. Inquiries may also be addressed to the Director of Graduate Study, Department of Comparative Literature, Box E, Brown University, Providence, Rhode Island 02912 or by&nbsp;<a href="mailto:comp_lit@brown.edu" rel="nofollow">emailing our Department</a>.</p>
<h3>Languages</h3>
<p>The completion of the program requires development of language skills sufficient for advanced work in three literatures. The languages selected are chosen with a view to their appropriateness to the student's areas of special interest. The Department's major competence is in literary expression in western languages. However, our growing programs in classical and modern Chinese, Japanese, and Arabic provide opportunity for students trained in these languages. In addition, the study of both ancient and modern Hebrew is available through Judaic Studies. In at least one of the foreign languages presented, near native proficiency in speaking and writing in the case of a modern language will be expected. See the Language Competence section of the Graduate Procedures for specific requirements.</p>
<h3>Course Work</h3>
<p>Students entering with the B.A. will normally take 15 advanced literature courses and graduate seminars, spread over three years in the proportions respectively of 8, 6, 1, two or three of which may be individual work supervised by a staff member. In special circumstances students may obtain up to a year of course credit for graduate work done at other institutions. Students who enter already having completed some advanced work may be asked to proceed more quickly. Individual programs are worked out in consultation with the Director of Graduate Study to include:</p>
<ul>
<li>a substantial core of graduate seminars whose primary department listing is in Comparative Literature, at least one per semester during the first two years of study</li>
<li>a substantial core of courses primarily in one national literature along with significant related work in at least two others. Courses taken in the second and third literatures must include a minimum of two regularly scheduled graduate seminars (or 100 level where appropriate with approval of the Director of Graduate Study)</li>
<li>a spread of courses comprising work in all three major genres (poetry, drama, narrative) and covering a significant range of distinct cultural epochs (medieval, romantic, modern, and so forth)</li>
<li>some work in the area of literary theory, literary criticism, or literary translation.</li>
<li>if pertinent, courses relating literature to other fields of inquiry or expression; for example, linguistics, philosophy, psychology, history, music, or the visual arts</li>
</ul>
<h3>Comparative Project</h3>
<p>While the historical "coverage" by itself is not the aim of comparative literature, the Department does require a major literature examination just before the fifth semester which has coverage as a partial goal, in order to demonstrate the student's professional capacities in her/his national literature. It also requires a written comparative project, to be submitted in writing and presented orally during the sixth semester. This project allows the student to treat work from more than one literary tradition, and may become part of the dissertation. A topic will be chosen in consultation with a faculty comittee and worked out with the Director of Graduate Study. It is expected that the comparative project will be completed and approved, and the student advanced to doctoral candidacy, by the end of the third year. See Graduate Program Procedures.</p>
<h3>The Dissertation</h3>
<p>By the end of the third year of study the student is expected to select the area of focus for the dissertation. The student will ordinarily work under the close supervision of a member of the Comparative Literature faculty; the thesis must also be approved by two other readers, one of whom may be from outside the Department.</p>
<h3>Teaching Positions</h3>
<p>Training and experience in teaching are major features of our doctoral program in Comparative Literature. The Department makes every attempt to provide its graduate students with teaching experience at Brown in undergraduate courses suited to the graduate student's interest. Teaching assistants work under the direct supervision of members of the professorial staff. At least two years of work as a teaching assistant are required for the Ph.D. The Department will keep students informed of positions as they become available in pertinent departments and at other institutions, and will assist them in presenting their candidacy for such positions.</p>
<p>Advanced graduate students who have made substantial progress on their dissertations, who can document their success in the classroom, and who have the support of a faculty member willing to serve as a classroom mentor are invited to submit a proposal to teach an undergraduate course (below 1000-level) in the department. The proposal consists of a 200-word course description, a syllabus, a C.V. that includes a listing of teaching experience, and notes of support from the dissertation advisor and faculty mentor. It is due to the Director of Graduate Studies no later than October 31 of the academic year before the class is taught. The proposals will be evaluated by the department’s Graduate Committee on their merits; no more than two will be approved in any year; final approval will depend on the curricular needs of the department as determined by the Chair.</p>
<h3>The M.A. Degree</h3>
<p>The Department does not admit terminal M.A. candidates, nor does it require a Master's degree as a prerequisite for the doctorate. Students who are not recommended to proceed in the doctoral program at the end of the first year may take an M.A. by completing one year of full-time course work in residence (normally four courses each semester), satisfying two of the three language requirements for the Ph.D., and completing a Master's thesis consisting of an essay of 50-60 pages on a comparative subject. Alternatively, a student will receive the M.A. upon completion of the comparative project and advancement to doctoral candidacy.</p>
<h2>Financing and Support</h2>
<p>Students are supported by a fellowship in their first year.&nbsp; Teaching responsibilities, and support in the form of a teaching assistantship, begin in the second year and continue through the fourth.&nbsp; We offer a dissertation fellowship in the fifth year and a number of avenues to sixth-year funding, including interdisciplinary opportunities at Brown’s Centers and Institutes and stipend support from the department and the Graduate School.&nbsp; In special cases, the Graduate School may cover tuition and health costs, but not stipend support, beyond the sixth year.&nbsp; Please refer to the&nbsp;<a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/gradschool/financing-support">Graduate School</a>&nbsp;web site for further information about financing graduate study at Brown.</p>
<p><span style="color:#2f2a20">Related Departments and Programs</span></p>
<p>The language and literature offerings at Brown include the following: Classics (including Greek, Latin and Sanskrit), English, French Studies, German Studies, Hebrew, Hispanic Studies, Italian Studies, Portuguese and Brazilian, Arabic, Chinese, Japanese, Linguistics, Slavic Languages.</p>

<section id="audience-gateways">
  <div class="marginator">
    <h6>Information For:</h6>

  <div id="content">
    <div class="marginator">
                  <div class="columns main-columns">
        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Graduate Program Procedures</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-195" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h2>I. Language Competence</h2>
<p>Students do advanced work in the original language in at least three  literatures as demonstrated by the successful completion of at least two  courses in each of those literatures at the 2000 course level. In  certain cases, appropriate 1000 level courses may safisty this  requirement with the permission of the Director of Graduate Study. This  requirement is to be satisfied by the end of semester four. Students  whose linguistic competence requires them to take language courses below  the 1000 level may do so in consultation with the Director of Graduate  Study, but those courses will not count toward the course requirements  for the Ph.D. Normally at least the first foreign language requirement  will be satisfied through advanced courses during the first year.</p>
<p>A standard of professional competence should be maintained within the  area of likely specialization. For example, a student intending to  specialize in the medieval period will offer an appropriate language  competence, Anglo-Saxon or Middle English for English, Middle High  German for German, Old French or Provençal for French, etc. Students  concentrating in Classics must offer both Latin and Greek; German is  also recommended. For those with specialties in medieval and  renaissance, Latin is recommended. Ordinarily two languages from the  same group -- Romance, Slavic, Germanic (exclusive of English) -- will  not be selected.</p>
<h2>II. Major Literature Exam</h2>
<p>Students will take an oral examination on their designated major literature by late September of Semester V.</p>
<h3>A. Timing and Committee</h3>
<p>Students must declare the major literature on which they will be  examined by September 30 of the second year (i.e., Semester III). During  the spring semester of their second year, students will take at least  two regular courses and may elect one section of COLT 2980, devoted to  preparation for the major literature examination, as the required third  course. During the second year, the Director of Graduate Study will form  an exam committee in consultation with students. Committees will be  made up of members of the Comparative Literature faculty; a professor  who is not a member of the Department may serve on examination  committees at the discretion of the Director of Graduate Study in those  cases where a specialty not covered by departmental faculty is  presented. All students presenting a given literature at the same time  will be examined by one committee. The final reading list, described in  the next paragraph, is to be submitted to the Director of Graduate Study  by March 1 of the second year.</p>
<h3>B. Scope of the Examination</h3>
<p>The examination will be based on a list of 30 items covering the  chronological range of the literature in question. An item generally  consists of several works by the author in question, roughly three  novels or prose works, eight to ten plays, or two dozen or so poems. The  texts for each item are to be chosen in consultation with the  examination committee.</p>
<p>Major literature reading lists for Classical Greek, Latin, English  and U.S. literature, French, German, Portuguese, and Spanish are  available in the Comparative Literature office as well as on this  website. Students presenting lists in other languages and literatures  will consult with the Director of Graduate Study and appropriate faculty  in formulating reading lists.</p>
<h3>C. The Oral Examination</h3>
<p>The examination will be administered in English by the three members  of the student's committee. It will last no more than two hours.  Questions will draw on the reading list, and will be of an appropriate  theoretical, historical, or textual nature. Passing will be decided by  majority vote taken directly after the examination.  If a student fails  the examination, one further attempt will be permitted, ordinarily  during the following semester. Normally students will either pass or  fail the examination as a whole, but in the event that the committee  determines a student has failed a portion of the examination,  the  committee may require that the student repeat that portion of the exam  at a later date. Ordinarily the re-examination will take place the  following semester.</p>
<h2>III. Comparative Project</h2>
<p>The comparative project provides an opportunity to begin focused  research leading to the dissertation, and to gain experience in  professional writing, oral presentation, and discussion comparable to a  conference situation or job interview.</p>
<h3>A. Timing and Committee</h3>
<p>After passing the major literature examination, students will begin  formulating a topic for the comparative project during Semester V and  complete it during the third year. The topic for the comparative project  will be worked out in consultation with the student's preliminary  doctoral committee, consisting of three faculty members, to be  established by the student in consultation with the Director of Graduate  Study.</p>
<p>Students must declare their intention of fulfilling the comparative  project requirement to the Director of Graduate Study and the Prelim  Committee at least eight weeks prior to the oral examination. In  Semester VI, students will submit the comparative project to their  examination committee in written form. A revised draft, to be considered  by the examination committee for the oral defense, must be submitted by  April 15th.</p>
<h3>B. The Comparative Project</h3>
<p>The comparative project is an article length essay which may or may  not become a part of the dissertation. As stated above, the project  provides an opportunity to begin focused research on subjects possibly  leading to the dissertation.</p>
<h3>C. Oral Presentation of the Comparative Project</h3>
<p>Approval of the written project must be given by each member of the  committee in writing to the Director of Graduate Study before the oral  examination can be scheduled.</p>
<p>The candidate will give a brief presentation at the beginning of the examination, situating the project, and where appropriate, indicating how it might relate to the dissertation.</p>
<h2>IV. Ph.D. Thesis</h2>
<p>A. Students should submit the doctoral thesis prospectus to their  dissertation committee and the Graduate Committee by September 30 of  their fourth year (i.e., Semester VII).<br /> <br /> B. Typically, the prospectus will include a clear statement of the  objective of the dissertation, a discussion of the issues involved, a  selective account of the key sources (primary, secondary, and  methodological), and a chapter outline. Normally, it may run to about 10  pages; it will not usually exceed 15 pages.<br /> <br /> C. After the prospectus is filed, members of the Graduate Committee,  and other members of the faculty, may offer comments on it.<br /> <br /> D. If a student changes topic or advisor, a new prospectus may need to  be filed; this decision falls within the discretion of the dissertation  advisor.</p>
<h2>V. Time-table for completion of the Ph.D.</h2>
<p>The requirements stated above are intended to provide both  comparatist training and preparation in one major literature (to  maximize placement possibilities) within a reasonable time (five years)  and with financial support. An effort will be made to provide teaching  experience not only in Comparative Literature, but also within an  appropriate foreign language or English, or Modern Culture and Media.  The timetable envisioned allows for the completion of course work, the  major literature examination and a comparative project by the end of the  third year, submission of the doctoral thesis proposal at the beginning  of the fourth, and one or two years of work to complete the doctoral  thesis.</p>
<p>The department makes every effort to provide, but cannot guarantee,  support for five years through teaching assistantships and proctorships  in Comparative Literature and other appropriate departments. Both  fellowships and teaching assistantships are contingent on the timely and  successful completion of course work and examinations. Support for  students entering with substantial advanced work or an MA will normally  be three years with the possibility of a fourth year. In the case of a  student failing the major literature examination, provision will be made  for a second try. Except for that eventuality or in the case of serious  illness, this timetable should be adhered to in order for support to  continue. First year students will be reviewed at the end of Semester I  and again after Semester II. Subsequently all students are reviewed at  year's end and are notified in writing of their progress.</p>

        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Reading Lists</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-196" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h2>Ph.D. Reading List in English with American Literature</h2>
<ul>
<li><em>Beowulf</em></li>
<li>Chaucer</li>
<li>Renaissance lyric (Skelton, Wyatt, Surrey, Spenser, Sidney, Jonson, Donne)</li>
<li>Shakespeare</li>
<li>Renaissance drama &nbsp;(Marlowe, Jonson, Dekker, Middleton, Webster)</li>
<li>Spenser and Milton</li>
<li>selected early British and American prose (Bunyan, Mather, Swift, Franklin, Edwards)</li>
<li>Richardson, Fielding, Sterne</li>
<li>British and American verse of the 17th and 18th century (Bradstreet, Dryden, Taylor, Pope, Johnson, Freneau, Wheatley)</li>
<li>English Romantic verse (Blake, Byron, Coleridge, Wordsworth, Keats, Shelley)</li>
<li>Scott, Cooper, Twain</li>
<li>Longfellow, Whitman, Dickinson</li>
<li>Emerson, Poe, Thoreau</li>
<li>Hawthorne and Melville</li>
<li>Austen, George Eliot, and Dickens</li>
<li>English Victorian literature (the Brownings, the Brontës, Carlyle, Tennyson, Robert, Wilde)</li>
<li>Stowe, Douglass, Du Bois</li>
<li>James and Faulkner</li>
<li>Chopin, Cather, Wharton</li>
<li>Hardy and Conrad</li>
<li>Joyce and Woolf</li>
<li>Frost, Stevens, Pound, Eliot</li>
<li>Dos Passos, Fitzgerald, Hemingway</li>
<li>Welty and O’Connor</li>
<li>Literature of the Harlem renaissance (also Schuyler)</li>
<li>Ellison, Wright, Morrison</li>
<li>Postmodern fiction (Nabokov, Gaddis, Barthelme, DeLillo, Pynchon)</li>
<li>British and American poetry of the 20th and 21st century</li>
<li>drama of the 20th and 21st century (Shaw, O’Neill, Beckett, Williams, Miller, Albee, Pinter)</li>
<li>Anglophone fiction of the 20th and 21st century (Rhys, Achebe, Naipaul, Soyinka, Rushdie, Kincaid, Coetzee)</li>
</ul>
<h2>Ph.D. Reading List in French Literature</h2>
<ul>
<li><em>La Chanson de Roland</em></li>
<li>Marie de France</li>
<li>Chrétien de Troyes</li>
<li>Villon&nbsp;</li>
<li>Rabelais</li>
<li>Montaigne</li>
<li>Selected poetry of Ronsard, DuBellay, Scève, Labé <strong>&nbsp;</strong></li>
<li>Corneille</li>
<li>Molière</li>
<li>Racine</li>
<li>Pascal&nbsp;</li>
<li>Madame de Lafayette</li>
<li>Montesquieu</li>
<li>Selected works by Charrière, Graffigny, Gouges, Staël</li>
<li>Diderot</li>
<li>Voltaire</li>
<li>Rousseau</li>
<li>Hugo and/or Sand</li>
<li>Stendhal and/or Zola</li>
<li>Balzac</li>
<li>Flaubert</li>
<li>Selected poetry of Baudelaire, Rimbaud, Mallarmé</li>
<li>Proust<em> </em></li>
<li>Beckett</li>
<li>Gide and/or Camus and/or Céline</li>
<li>selected modern poetry, such as Apollinaire, Breton, Éluard, Ponge, Césaire, Senghor, Bonnefoy<strong>, </strong>Hocquard, Roubaud, Deguy <strong>&nbsp;</strong></li>
<li>Césaire, “Discours sur le colonialisme”; Fanon, <em>Peau noire, masques blancs</em>;<em> </em>Chamoiseau et al, “Éloge de la créolité”; and literary works selected from among the following authors: Condé, Chamoiseau, Pineau, Schwarz-Bart, Ollivier, Laferrière<strong>&nbsp;</strong></li>
<li>Laâbi, “Réalités et dilemmes de la culture nationale I &amp; II”; Haddad, “Les Zéros tournent en rond”; Memmi, <em>Portrait du colonisé, précédé du portrait du colonisateur</em>;<em> </em>and works selected from among the following authors: &nbsp;Kateb Yacine, Chraïbi, Djebar, Ben Jelloun, Kane, Mariama Bâ, Camara Laye, Sembène, Kourouma<strong>&nbsp;</strong></li>
<li>selected works of mid-century &amp; contemporary French fiction, such as Duras, Sarraute, Robbe-Grillet, Perec, Modiano, Le Clézio, Michon, Quignard, Toussaint, Redonnet, Cixous, Chevillard, Bon, Guibert, Rouaud, Ernaux, Houellebecq, NDiaye, Darrieussecq, Sebbar<strong>&nbsp;</strong></li>
<li>selected critical/ philosophical texts, such as Bataille, Beauvoir, Sartre, Lévi-Strauss, Blanchot, Barthes, Fanon, Foucault, Irigaray, Derrida, Deleuze, Glissant, and Mernissi.</li>
</ul>
<h2>Ph.D. Reading List in English Literature</h2>
<ul>
<li><em>Beowulf</em>, <em>The Wanderer</em>, <em>The Seafarer</em>, <em>Dream of the Rood</em></li>
<li>Chaucer</li>
<li><em>Sir Gawain and the Green Knight</em>, Langland</li>
<li>Spenser</li>
<li>Shakespeare</li>
<li>Milton</li>
<li>medieval and early modern drama (<em>Everyman</em>, Marlowe, Jonson, Kyd, Middleton)</li>
<li>early modern verse &nbsp;(Crashaw, Donne, Herbert, Jonson, Marvell, Marlowe, Sidney, Skelton, Vaughan, Wyatt)</li>
<li>early modern prose (Bacon, Burton, Browne, Donne, More, Nashe, Tyndale)</li>
<li>Dryden and Pope</li>
<li>Addison and Steele, Johnson</li>
<li>Richardson and Fielding</li>
<li>Defoe, Sterne, and Smollett</li>
<li>Scott and Austen</li>
<li>Gaskell, the Brontës</li>
<li>Blake, Coleridge, Wordsworth</li>
<li>Byron, Keats, Shelley</li>
<li>Dickens, Thackeray, Eliot</li>
<li>Victorian poetry (Arnold, the Brownings, Hopkins, Housman, the Rossettis)</li>
<li>Victorian prose (Carlyle, Pater, Ruskin, Newman)</li>
<li>Hardy and Conrad</li>
<li>James</li>
<li>Yeats, Pound, Eliot</li>
<li>Woolf and Lawrence</li>
<li>Joyce</li>
<li>postmodern fiction</li>
<li>British poetry of the 20th and 21st century</li>
<li>American poetry of the 20th and 21st century</li>
<li>drama of the 20th and 21st century (Shaw, O’Neill, Beckett, Williams, Miller, Albee, Pinter)</li>
<li>Anglophone fiction of the 20th and 21st century (Achebe, Coetzee, Kincaid, Naipaul, Rhys, Rushdie, Soyinka)</li>
</ul>
<h2>Ph.D. Reading Lists in Latin and Greek</h2>
<h3>Latin Required</h3>
<ul>
<li>Apuleius, Metamorphoses, books 4-6</li>
<li>Caesar, Bellum Civile</li>
<li>Catullus, Carmina</li>
<li>Cicero, Letters (ed. How or Tyrell or Stockton), in Catilinam I-IV, pro Archia, pro Archia, pro Milone, pro Sulla, pro Caelio, de Amicitia, de Re Publica, de Oratore.</li>
<li>Ennius, Fragments</li>
<li>Horace, Odes books 1-3, Epodes, Satires, Ars Poetica</li>
<li>Juvenal, Satires 1, 3, 5, 10, 15.</li>
<li>Livy, Ab Urbe Condita, books 1, 5, 22-22</li>
<li>Lucretius, de Rerum Natura</li>
<li>Martial, ed. Post</li>
<li>Ovid, Metamorphoses, Ars Amatoria.</li>
<li>Petronius, Cena Trimalchionis</li>
<li>Plautus, Menaechmi, Miles Gloriosus</li>
<li>Pliny the Younger, Letters (any 25 in Poteat or Sherwin-White or Wescott)</li>
<li>Propertius, Carmina</li>
<li>Quintilian, Institutio Oratoria, book 10</li>
<li>Sallust, Catilina</li>
<li>Seneca the younger, Hippolytus</li>
<li>Tacitus, Agricola, Annales books I-IV</li>
<li>Tibullus, Carmina books 1-2</li>
<li>Vergil, Eclogues, Georgics, Aeneid</li>
</ul>
<h3>Latin Additional</h3>
<ul>
<li>Accius, Fragments</li>
<li>Ammianus Marcellinus, Rerum Gestarum Libri</li>
<li>Apuleius, Metamorphoses, Apologia, Florida, De Magia</li>
<li>Augustine, Confessions</li>
<li>Aulus Gellius, Attic Nights</li>
<li>Boethius, Consolation of Philosophy</li>
<li>Caesar, Bellum Gallicum</li>
<li>Cato, De Agri Cultura</li>
<li>Cicero, pro Balbo, pro Milone, pro Flacco, pro Lege Manilia, Phillippics 1, 2, in Pisonem, pro Rabirio, in Verrem, pro S. Roscio Amerino, de Finibus, de Natura Deorum, de Officiis, de Senectute, Tusculan Disputations, Brutus</li>
<li>Claudianus, in Rufinum</li>
<li>Horace, Epistles</li>
<li>Juvenal, Satires</li>
<li>Livy, Ab Urbe Condita</li>
<li>Lucan, Pharsalia</li>
<li>Lucilius, Fragments</li>
<li>Martial, Epigrammata</li>
<li>Nepos, de Viris Illustribus</li>
<li>Ovid, Amores, Tristia, Fasti, Remedia Amoris</li>
<li>Persius, Satires</li>
<li>Petronius, Satiricon</li>
<li>Plautus, Mostellaria, Pseudolus, Rudens, Trinummus, Amphitruo etc.</li>
<li>Pliny the Elder, Historia Naturalis</li>
<li>Pliny the Youngerz Letters</li>
<li>Quintilian, Institutio Oratoria</li>
<li>Sallust, Jugurtha</li>
<li>Scriptores Historiae Augustae</li>
<li>Seneca the Elder, Suasoriae</li>
<li>Seneca the Younger, de Consolatione, de Ira, Quaestiones Naturales, Epistolae Morales, Apocolocyntosis, Hercules Furens, Medea, Octavia Praetextata</li>
<li>Statius, Thebaid</li>
<li>Suetonius, de Vita Caesarum</li>
<li>Tacitus, Dialogus de Oratoribus, Historiae, Germania</li>
<li>Terence, Adelphoe, Eunuchus, Hecyra, Heauton Timorumenos</li>
<li>Tibullus, Carmina</li>
<li>Varro, de Lingua Latina</li>
<li>Vitruvius, de Architectura</li>
</ul>
<h3>Greek Required</h3>
<ul>
<li>Aeschylus, Agamemnon, Choephoroi, Eumendides, Prometheus</li>
<li>Alcaeus, Poems in Page, Sappho and Alcaeus</li>
<li>Alcman, Poem in Campbell, Greek Lyric Poetry</li>
<li>Andocides, On the Mysteries</li>
<li>Antiphon, On the Murder of Herodes.</li>
<li>Archilochus, All poems in Diehls, Anth. Lyr. Graec. and Fragments in Lasserre &amp; Bonnard, Archiloque: Fragments, 1958.</li>
<li>Aristophanes, Acharnians, Clouds, Lysistrata, Frogs.</li>
<li>Aristotle, Poetics, Constitution of the Athenians.</li>
<li>Bacchylides, all poems.</li>
<li>Callimachus, Hymns.</li>
<li>Demosthenes, Philippics, Olynthiacs.</li>
<li>Euripides, Alcestis, Bacchae, Medea, Hippolytus. Heraclitus, fragments in Diels-Kranz, Vorsokratiker. Herodotus, 1, 2, 7, 8, 9.</li>
<li>Hesiod, Works &amp; Days, Theogony.</li>
<li>Homer, Iliad, Odyssey, Hymn to Demeter.</li>
<li>Lysias, Against Eratosthenes.</li>
<li>Menander, Dyskolos.</li>
<li>Pindar, Olympian Odes, Pythian Odes.</li>
<li>Plato, Apology, Crito, Phaedo, Symposium, Gorgias, Republic, books I, III, VI &amp; X.</li>
<li>Plutarch, Solon, Perikles.</li>
<li>Sappho, Poems in Page, Sappho &amp; Alcaeus.</li>
<li>Simonides, Poems in Campbell, Greek Lyric Poetry.</li>
<li>Sophocles, Ajax, Antigone, Oedipus Coloneus, Oedipus Tyrannus.</li>
<li>Theocritus, Id. 1, 2, 7, 11.</li>
<li>Thucydides, History, books 1-2, 4-5.26, 6-7.</li>
<li>Xenophon, Anabasis.</li>
</ul>

<h2>Ph.D. Reading List in Spanish Literature</h2>
<ul>

        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Undergraduate Program</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
                                                  <div class="clear-block"> 
<article id="node-12" class="main-article node">








  <div class="content">
        <h3>Program Overview</h3>
<p>The undergraduate programs for concentration in Comparative Literature enable students to study literature in cross-cultural perspectives. The aim of the program is to encourage students to study a varied and illustrative range of literary topics rather than the total development of a single literary tradition.<br />&nbsp;<br />Concentrators in Comparative Literature should be willing to try different approaches to literature and to examine literary and aesthetic problems with sustained curiosity. Comparative Literature differs from other literature concentrations largely through its international focus and its broad-gauged view of art and culture in which the study of languages is combined with the analysis of literature and literary theory.</p>
<h3>Concentration Requirements</h3>
<p>True to the spirit of Brown’s New Curriculum, a concentration in Comparative Literature affords great academic freedom. Although the Comparative Literature concentration requirements that follow give shape and rigor to the major, they allow advanced courses in any literature department at Brown to count for concentration credit. Courses in related fields, when vitally relevant to an individual’s Comparative Literature studies, may also receive concentration credit. In other words, by no means are our concentrators limited to courses in the Comparative Literature department, though all are encouraged to take our courses because they generally enact cross-cultural work.</p>
<p>It is important to note that English is commonly one of the languages that students apply to a concentration in Comparative Literature. Yet, basically any language--ancient or modern--supported at Brown may form part of a Comparative Literature concentration program. While our courses are offered in English (students may read texts in their original languages), concentrators also take courses in the national literature departments of their chosen languages. We encourage and help concentrators to study abroad.</p>
<p>What follows are the three possible tracks of the concentration along with normative timetable expectations for each.&nbsp;<em>&nbsp;</em></p>
<p><span class="style16">The expectation for students working in a non-European language or languages is that they will at least complete the equivalent of three years of college-level language study and, before they graduate, utilize their language proficiency in at least two courses agreed upon with their concentration advisor.</span></p>
<h3><strong>Track 1: Concentration in Comparative Literature with Two Languages<br /></strong></h3>
<ul>
<li>Complete prerequisite(s) for taking 1000-level courses in your two languages by Semester V (students working in non-European languages may be allowed more latitude; be sure to consult a concentration advisor about constructing an individualized plan).</li>
<li>Comparative Literature 1210, Introduction to the Theory of Literature.</li>
<li>Ten advanced literature courses (generally 1000-level courses), including Comparative Literature 1210 and:
<ul>
<li>At least two courses in the literature of each of your languages, and the remainder drawn chiefly from among the offerings of Comparative Literature and English and other national literature departments. In most cases, courses need to be taught in the language of the literature.</li>
<li>One course chiefly devoted to EACH of the three major literary genres: poetry, drama and narrative.</li>
<li>One literature course chiefly devoted to each of three of the following five historical periods: (a) Antiquity, (b) Middle Ages, (c) Renaissance/Early Modern, (d) Enlightenment, (e) Modern. Please note that the 19th, 20th, and 21st centuries count as one period, the Modern Period.<br /><a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Tracks%201-2%20checklist%20%28Nov%202015%29_0.docx">Concentration Track 1 Requirements Checklist </a></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h3>Track 2: Concentration in Comparative Literature with Three Languages<strong><br /></strong></h3>
<ul>
<li>Complete prerequisite(s) for taking 1000-level courses in two of your languages by Semester V (students working in non-European languages may be allowed more latitude; be sure to consult a concentration advisor about constructing an individualized plan).</li>
<li>Complete the same requirement for your third language before Semester VII (the above proviso for students working in non-European languages also holds here).</li>
<li>Comparative Literature 1210, Introduction to the Theory of Literature.</li>
<li>TEN advanced literature courses (generally 1000-level courses), including Comparative Literature 1210 and:
<ul>
<li>At least two courses in the literature of each of your languages, and the remainder drawn chiefly from among the offerings of Comparative Literature and English and other national literature departments. In most cases, courses need to be taught in the language of the literature.</li>
<li>One course chiefly devoted to each of the three major literary genres: poetry, drama and narrative.</li>
<li>One literature course chiefly devoted to each of three of the following five historical periods: (a) Antiquity, (b) Middle Ages, (c) Renaissance/Early Modern, (d) Enlightenment, (e) Modern. Please note that the 19th, 20th, and 21st centuries count as one period, the Modern Period.<br /><a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Tracks%201-2%20checklist%20%28Nov%202015%29_0.docx">Concentration Track 2 Requirements Checklist</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>
<h3><strong>Track 3: Concentration in Literary Translation<br /></strong></h3>
<ul>
<li>Complete prerequisite(s) for taking 1000-level courses in your two languages before Semester V (students working in non-European languages may be allowed more latitude; be sure to consult a concentration advisor about constructing an individualized plan).</li>
<li>Comparative Literature 1210, Introduction to the Theory of Literature.</li>
<li>Comparative Literature 1710 (Comparative Literature 2720 strongly urged).</li>
<li>One course or more in Linguistics, drawn from among these courses: Cognitive Science 0410, Anthropology 0800, English 1210, Hispanic Studies 1210, or an acceptable substitute.</li>
<li>Five or six advanced literature courses (generally 1000-level courses), including Comparative Literature 1210 and:
<ul>
<li>At least two courses in the literature of each of your languages, and the remainder drawn chiefly from among the offerings of Comparative Literature and English and other national literature departments. In most cases, courses need to be taught in the language of the literature.</li>
<li>One course chiefly devoted to each of the three major literary genres: poetry, drama and narrative.</li>
<li>One literature course chiefly devoted to each of three of the following five historical periods: (a) Antiquity, (b) Middle Ages, (c) Renaissance/Early Modern, (d) Enlightenment, (e) Modern. Please note that the 19th, 20th, and 21st centuries count as one period, the Modern Period.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>Two workshops or more in Creative Writing.</li>
<li>A senior project to consist of: 1. A substantial work in translation (length will vary depending on language and genre); 2. A critical introduction outlining the method used and specific problems encountered, and commenting on the history of the original work together with other translations, if any. For the thesis, the student may register for COLT 1990, which will be taken in addition to the ten required courses listed above. Successful completion of the thesis constitutes Honors. (See Guidelines for Honors Theses).<br /><a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Track%203%20checklist%20%282.4.2016%29.docx">Concentration Track 3 Requirements Checklist</a></li>
</ul>
<p>Note:<br /><span class="small">Students in the literature tracks not taking Honors are urged, but not required, to complete a senior essay, which may be less extensive in scope and length than the Honors thesis (see </span><a class="small" href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/undergraduate-program/honors-thesis">Guidelines for Honors Theses</a><span class="small"> in Comparative Literature) but which should constitute an integration of some aspect of their study.</span></p>
<p>Course options for fulfilling concentration requirements:&nbsp;<br />&nbsp;<a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Reqs.%20Spring%202017.pdf" target="_blank">Spring 2017</a> |&nbsp;<a href="https://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Reqs%20Fall%202016.pdf">Fall 2016 </a>&nbsp;&nbsp;|&nbsp;<a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/Spring%202016_%20Con%20Reqs_0.pdf">Spring 2016</a> &nbsp;|&nbsp;<a href="http://brown.edu/academics/comparative-literature/sites/brown.edu.academics.comparative-literature/files/uploads/course%20options%20for%20fulfilling%20concentration%20requirements%20Fall%202015.pdf">Fall 2015</a>&nbsp;&nbsp;</p>
<h2>Credits</h2>
<ul class="style16">
<li>2 courses per semester of study abroad may be applied to the concentration, up to a total of 4 courses (for two semesters abroad). Please discuss study abroad plans with your advisor.<strong><br /> </strong> </li>
<li>A maximum of 5 courses from external venues (study abroad; transfer credits from other institutions, including summer study) may be applied to the concentration.</li>
<li>In accordance with University policy, double concentrators are allowed a maximum overlap of 2 courses between concentrations.</li>
</ul>
<h2>Variation on the Basic Program</h2>
<p>It is possible for a student to accommodate a junior year abroad within his/her concentration program, provided that he/she plans for it early enough. In certain cases, a freshman may wish to consider spending his/her sophomore year abroad.</p>
<p>Joint or double concentration programs may also be arranged if steps are taken early enough with Comparative Literature and the other department. The same is true of programs combining a concentration in Comparative Literature with a teaching certificate in English or a modern language. A student interested in such a program should consult the advisor in the <a href="http://www.brown.edu/academics/education/" target="_blank">Education Department</a> and the advisor in Comparative Literature as early as possible (preferably by Semester V).</p>
<p><strong><span class="highlight">&nbsp;</span><br /></strong></p>
  </div>

        <h1 class="article-title  with-tabs">Comparative Literature Career Forum</h1>                        <div id="body-content" class="second-column column main-column">
